### Introduction

Mao Zedong, the founding father of the People's Republic of China, played a pivotal role in shaping the country's foreign policy. His leadership spanned from the establishment of the People's Republic in 1949 until his death in 1976. Mao's foreign policy was characterized by a blend of ideological fervor, strategic pragmatism, and a deep-seated suspicion of Western powers. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of Mao's foreign policy, exploring his key decisions, strategies, and their impact on China's international relations.

Mao's foreign policy was heavily influenced by his Marxist-Leninist ideology. He believed in the concept of proletarian internationalism, which emphasized the unity of the world's working class against the exploitative capitalist system. This ideology guided Mao's approach to international relations, particularly his relations with the Soviet Union and the United States.

Despite his ideological leanings, Mao was also a shrewd strategist. He understood the importance of maintaining good relations with neighboring countries, particularly those bordering the Soviet Union. This led to his policy of "leaning to one side" in foreign affairs, a strategy that aimed to balance relations with both the Soviet Union and the United States.

However, Mao's foreign policy was not without its challenges. His ideological rigidity often led to conflicts with other communist powers, particularly the Soviet Union. His decision to launch the disastrous Great Leap Forward and the subsequent Cultural Revolution further strained China's relations with the Soviet Union and the United States.

This chapter will explore these aspects of Mao's foreign policy in detail, providing a comprehensive understanding of his approach to international relations. It will also examine the lasting impact of Mao's policies on China's foreign relations, offering insights into the country's current foreign policy strategies.




### Section: 4.1 Thought:

#### 4.1a Overview of Mao's Philosophy

Mao Zedong's thought was deeply rooted in Marxist philosophy, particularly the concept of dialectical materialism. Mao's interpretation of Marxism, however, was heavily influenced by his own experiences and the unique circumstances of China. This led to the development of a distinct Maoist philosophy that emphasized the centrality of contradiction and struggle in the pursuit of revolutionary change.

Mao's philosophy was shaped by his early exposure to Marxist thought during his studies at Peking University. He was particularly influenced by the works of Karl Marx, Friedrich Engels, and Vladimir Lenin. Mao's interpretation of Marxism, however, was heavily influenced by his own experiences and the unique circumstances of China. This led to the development of a distinct Maoist philosophy that emphasized the centrality of contradiction and struggle in the pursuit of revolutionary change.

One of the key concepts in Mao's philosophy was the concept of contradiction. Mao saw the world as a constant struggle between opposing forces, with the ultimate goal being the resolution of these contradictions through revolutionary struggle. This concept was heavily influenced by Mao's reading of On Contradiction, a key text in Marxist philosophy that emphasizes the dialectical nature of reality.

Mao's philosophy also emphasized the role of the individual in the pursuit of revolutionary change. He saw the individual as the driving force behind historical change, and emphasized the importance of individual initiative and action in the pursuit of revolutionary goals. This emphasis on the individual was a departure from traditional Marxist thought, which emphasized the role of the collective in historical change.

Mao's philosophy also played a significant role in shaping his approach to foreign policy. His emphasis on contradiction and struggle led him to view the world in terms of opposing forces, with the United States and the Soviet Union as the two main antagonists. This led to Mao's policy of "leaning to one side" in foreign affairs, a strategy that aimed to balance relations with both the Soviet Union and the United States.

However, Mao's philosophy also led to conflicts with other communist powers, particularly the Soviet Union. His ideological rigidity often led to conflicts with other communist powers, particularly the Soviet Union. This was evident in the Sino-Soviet split of the 1960s, which was largely driven by ideological differences between Mao and Soviet leader Nikita Khrushchev.

In conclusion, Mao's philosophy played a significant role in shaping his approach to foreign policy. His emphasis on contradiction and struggle, as well as his emphasis on the individual, led to a unique approach to international relations that had a lasting impact on China's foreign policy.

#### 4.1b Mao's Thought and Foreign Policy

Mao's thought had a profound impact on China's foreign policy during his tenure as the leader of the Communist Party of China. His philosophy, heavily influenced by Marxist dialectical materialism, shaped his approach to international relations and strategy. 

Mao's emphasis on contradiction and struggle was reflected in his foreign policy. He saw the world as a constant struggle between opposing forces, with the ultimate goal being the resolution of these contradictions through revolutionary struggle. This led to a foreign policy that was characterized by a constant struggle for power and influence, with China often aligning itself with one power against another.

Mao's philosophy also emphasized the role of the individual in the pursuit of revolutionary change. This emphasis on the individual was reflected in his foreign policy, particularly in his approach to the United States and the Soviet Union. Mao saw these two superpowers as the main antagonists in the global struggle for power and influence. He believed that China could only achieve its goals by aligning itself with one of these powers against the other.

This led to Mao's policy of "leaning to one side" in foreign affairs, a strategy that aimed to balance relations with both the United States and the Soviet Union. This policy was a reflection of Mao's belief in the importance of individual initiative and action in the pursuit of revolutionary goals. By aligning itself with one power against the other, China could assert its own power and influence in the global arena.

However, Mao's philosophy also led to conflicts with other communist powers, particularly the Soviet Union. His ideological rigidity often led to conflicts with other communist powers, particularly the Soviet Union. This was evident in the Sino-Soviet split of the 1960s, which was largely driven by ideological differences between Mao and Soviet leader Nikita Khrushchev.

In conclusion, Mao's thought played a significant role in shaping China's foreign policy during his tenure as the leader of the Communist Party of China. His emphasis on contradiction and struggle, as well as his emphasis on the individual, shaped his approach to international relations and strategy. This had a profound impact on China's foreign policy, leading to both successes and failures in its pursuit of power and influence in the global arena.

#### 4.1c Mao's Thought and Domestic Policy

Mao's thought also had a significant impact on China's domestic policy during his tenure as the leader of the Communist Party of China. His philosophy, heavily influenced by Marxist dialectical materialism, shaped his approach to domestic affairs and strategy.

Mao's emphasis on contradiction and struggle was reflected in his domestic policy. He saw the world as a constant struggle between opposing forces, with the ultimate goal being the resolution of these contradictions through revolutionary struggle. This led to a domestic policy that was characterized by a constant struggle for power and influence, with Mao often pitting different factions and groups against each other.

Mao's philosophy also emphasized the role of the individual in the pursuit of revolutionary change. This emphasis on the individual was reflected in his domestic policy, particularly in his approach to the Cultural Revolution. Mao saw the Cultural Revolution as a struggle for power and influence, with the ultimate goal being the elimination of all vestiges of the old society and the establishment of a new, revolutionary society.

This led to Mao's policy of "leaning to one side" in domestic affairs, a strategy that aimed to balance relations between different factions and groups. This policy was a reflection of Mao's belief in the importance of individual initiative and action in the pursuit of revolutionary goals. By pitting different factions and groups against each other, Mao could assert his own power and influence in the domestic arena.

However, Mao's philosophy also led to conflicts and struggles within the Communist Party of China. His ideological rigidity often led to conflicts with other party members, particularly those who disagreed with his interpretation of Marxist philosophy. This was evident in the Cultural Revolution, which was largely driven by ideological differences between Mao and other party members.

In conclusion, Mao's thought played a significant role in shaping China's domestic policy during his tenure as the leader of the Communist Party of China. His emphasis on contradiction and struggle, as well as his emphasis on the individual, shaped his approach to domestic affairs and strategy. This had a profound impact on China's domestic politics, leading to both successes and failures in its pursuit of a revolutionary society.




### Section: 4.1b Influence on Chinese Foreign Policy

Mao Zedong's thought had a profound influence on Chinese foreign policy during his reign. His philosophy of contradiction and struggle shaped China's approach to international relations, particularly in its dealings with the United States and the Soviet Union.

Mao's emphasis on contradiction and struggle led him to view the world in terms of opposing forces, with the United States and the Soviet Union representing the two main contradictions in the international arena. This view was heavily influenced by Mao's interpretation of Marxist philosophy, which saw the world as a constant struggle between opposing forces.

In his book "On Contradiction," Mao wrote, "Contradiction is the key to dialectics. It is the fundamental concept that underlies all dialectical thinking and is the source of all dialectical movement." This concept of contradiction was central to Mao's approach to foreign policy, as he saw the world as a constant struggle between opposing forces.

Mao's emphasis on individual initiative and action also played a role in shaping Chinese foreign policy. He saw the individual as the driving force behind historical change, and this emphasis on individual initiative was reflected in China's approach to international relations. China's leaders often saw themselves as individual agents of historical change, and this view influenced their approach to foreign policy.

Mao's philosophy also had a significant impact on China's approach to the Korean War. Mao saw the war as a struggle between two opposing forces, with the United States and the Soviet Union representing the two main contradictions. This view influenced China's decision to intervene in the war, as it saw an opportunity to weaken the United States and strengthen its own position in the international arena.

In conclusion, Mao Zedong's thought had a profound influence on Chinese foreign policy during his reign. His emphasis on contradiction and struggle, as well as his emphasis on individual initiative and action, shaped China's approach to international relations and its dealings with the United States and the Soviet Union.




### Section: 4.1c Modern Interpretations

Mao Zedong's thought continues to be a subject of debate and interpretation in modern times. While some scholars argue that his philosophy was a product of his time and has little relevance in the current global political landscape, others see his ideas as still relevant and applicable to contemporary issues.

One modern interpretation of Mao's thought is the concept of "Maoism." Maoism is a political ideology that emphasizes the role of the individual in historical change and the constant struggle between opposing forces. It also advocates for a strong centralized state and a revolutionary approach to social and political issues.

Maoism has been used by various groups and movements around the world, particularly in Latin America and the United States. The Communist Party of Peru, for example, has been influenced by Maoism and has used his ideas to guide its revolutionary struggle. Similarly, the Communist Party USA has also been influenced by Mao's thought, particularly his emphasis on individual initiative and action.

Another modern interpretation of Mao's thought is the concept of "Maoist humanism." This interpretation emphasizes Mao's emphasis on human rights and his opposition to imperialism and colonialism. It also highlights his belief in the importance of individual initiative and action in historical change.

Maoist humanism has been used by scholars and activists to critique contemporary global issues, such as the exploitation of developing countries by Western powers. It also emphasizes the importance of individual agency in addressing these issues.

In conclusion, Mao Zedong's thought continues to be a subject of debate and interpretation in modern times. While some see his ideas as outdated and irrelevant, others argue that his philosophy is still relevant and applicable to contemporary issues. The concept of Maoism and Maoist humanism are just two examples of modern interpretations of Mao's thought.




### Subsection: 4.2a Mao's Foreign Policy

Mao Zedong's foreign policy was heavily influenced by his ideological beliefs and his desire to establish China as a global power. His approach to international relations was characterized by a combination of idealism and pragmatism, as he sought to balance China's economic and political interests with his personal beliefs.

#### Mao's Foreign Policy Objectives

Mao's foreign policy objectives were shaped by his belief in the inevitability of a global communist revolution. He saw China's role in this revolution as a leader of the developing world, and sought to establish strong alliances with other communist countries. This led to China's involvement in the Korean War and its support for North Vietnam and North Korea.

In addition to his ideological objectives, Mao also sought to establish China as a major economic and political power. This was evident in his pursuit of alliances with both the Soviet Union and the United States, as well as his efforts to establish China as a global leader in technology and industry.

#### Mao's Foreign Policy Strategies

Mao's foreign policy strategies were characterized by a combination of idealism and pragmatism. On one hand, he sought to establish strong alliances with other communist countries, as seen in his support for North Vietnam and North Korea. On the other hand, he also pursued pragmatic economic and political partnerships with both the Soviet Union and the United States.

Mao's foreign policy also reflected his personal beliefs and ideology. For example, his support for the Soviet Union was driven by his belief in the inevitability of a global communist revolution, while his pursuit of economic and political partnerships with the United States was driven by his desire to establish China as a major global power.

#### Mao's Foreign Policy Bureaucracy

Mao's foreign policy was largely controlled by a small group of advisors and officials, including Premier Zhou Enlai and Foreign Minister Chen Yi. This small group was responsible for making strategic decisions and handling security issues, with Mao ultimately making the final decisions.

Despite the small size of the foreign policy bureaucracy, Mao's personal involvement in foreign affairs led to a top-down approach to decision-making. This was evident in his frequent interventions in foreign policy matters, as well as his personal relationships with foreign leaders.

#### Mao's Foreign Policy Legacy

Mao's foreign policy had a significant impact on China's international relations and its global standing. His pursuit of strong alliances with other communist countries and his efforts to establish China as a major economic and political power have shaped China's foreign policy to this day.

However, Mao's foreign policy also had its flaws. His personal involvement in foreign affairs led to a lack of consistency and stability in China's foreign policy, as well as strained relationships with other countries. Despite these flaws, Mao's foreign policy remains a crucial aspect of Chinese foreign policy and continues to shape China's global role.





### Subsection: 4.2b Impact on International Relations

Mao's foreign policy had a significant impact on international relations during his reign. His ideological beliefs and pursuit of global communist revolution led to China's involvement in various conflicts, including the Korean War and its support for North Vietnam and North Korea. This involvement often strained China's relationships with other countries, particularly the United States and the Soviet Union.

Mao's pursuit of economic and political partnerships with both the Soviet Union and the United States also had a significant impact on international relations. His efforts to establish China as a major global power led to a complex web of alliances and rivalries, as other countries sought to maintain their own interests and influence.

Furthermore, Mao's foreign policy also had a lasting impact on China's relationships with other countries. His support for communist revolutions and his pursuit of global power have shaped China's foreign policy to this day, with a focus on maintaining strong alliances with other communist countries and establishing itself as a major economic and political power.

In conclusion, Mao's foreign policy had a profound impact on international relations during his reign and continues to shape China's foreign policy today. His ideological beliefs, pursuit of global power, and complex alliances and rivalries have left a lasting legacy on the international stage.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the foreign policy of China during the reign of Mao Zedong. We have seen how Mao's ideology of communism and his belief in the power of the masses shaped China's foreign relations during this period. We have also examined the impact of Mao's foreign policy on China's domestic affairs, particularly in terms of its economic development and international standing.

Mao's foreign policy was characterized by a desire to spread communism and challenge the dominance of Western powers. This led to China's involvement in various conflicts, such as the Korean War and the Vietnam War, as well as its support for communist movements in other countries. However, Mao's foreign policy also had its drawbacks, including economic isolation and strained relationships with other major powers.

Despite these challenges, Mao's foreign policy played a crucial role in shaping China's modern history. It laid the foundation for China's current foreign policy, which continues to prioritize economic development and non-interference in the internal affairs of other countries. As China continues to rise as a global power, it is important to understand the legacy of Mao's foreign policy and its impact on the country's international relations.

## Chapter: Chinese Foreign Policy: International Relations and Strategy

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will be examining the foreign policy of China during the reign of Deng Xiaoping. Deng Xiaoping was a Chinese politician who served as the paramount leader of China from 1978 to 1992. His leadership marked a significant shift in China's foreign policy, as he implemented a series of economic and political reforms that transformed the country into a major global power.

Deng's foreign policy was heavily influenced by his belief in the concept of "peaceful development," which emphasized the importance of maintaining a peaceful and stable international environment for China's economic growth and development. This approach was in stark contrast to the more aggressive and confrontational foreign policies of previous Chinese leaders, such as Mao Zedong and Zhou Enlai.

One of the key aspects of Deng's foreign policy was his emphasis on economic development and modernization. He believed that China's economic growth was crucial for its long-term stability and security, and therefore prioritized economic relations with other countries over political ideology. This led to China's integration into the global economy and its rise as a major economic power.

Another important aspect of Deng's foreign policy was his "one country, two systems" approach to governing Hong Kong and Macau. This policy allowed these territories to maintain their own economic and political systems while still being under Chinese sovereignty. This approach was seen as a way for China to modernize and develop while also maintaining its socialist ideology.

Overall, Deng's foreign policy was characterized by a shift towards a more pragmatic and economic-focused approach, as opposed to the more ideological and confrontational policies of previous Chinese leaders. This shift has had a lasting impact on China's foreign relations and continues to shape its approach to international relations today. In this chapter, we will explore the key events and decisions that shaped Deng's foreign policy and its impact on China's global standing.

His ideology of communism and his belief in the power of the masses shaped China's foreign policy during this period. We will explore the impact of Mao's foreign policy on China's economic development and international standing, as well as the challenges and opportunities it presented.

## Major issues

### Economics and trade

#### Taxation

Finance officials from 130 countries agreed on July 1, 2021, to plans for a new global minimum corporate tax rate. All the major economies agreed to pass national laws that would require corporations to pay at least 15% income tax in the countries they operate. This new policy would end the practice of locating world headquarters in small countries with very low taxation rates. Governments hope to recoup some of the lost revenue, estimated at $100billion to $240billion each year. The new system was promoted by the United States and the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD). Secretary-General Mathias Cormann of the OECD said, "This historic package will ensure that large multinational companies pay their fair share of tax everywhere." On July 10 the finance ministers of the G-20 all approved the plan.

#### Rise of China

China's economy saw continuous real annual GDP growth 5% to 10% since 1991, by far the highest rate in the world. This growth has allowed China to become the world's second-largest economy, surpassing Japan in 2010 and the United States in 2014. China's economic power has also led to its increasing influence in global affairs, particularly in international trade and investment.

### Politics and security

#### The Cold War

The Cold War, which lasted from the end of World War II in 1945 until the collapse of the Soviet Union in 1991, was a period of intense political and military tension between the United States and the Soviet Union. China, under Mao's leadership, aligned itself with the Soviet Union and became a key player in the Cold War. This alignment led to China's involvement in the Korean War and its support for communist revolutions in other countries, such as Vietnam and Cuba.

#### The Korean War

The Korean War, which lasted from 1950 to 1953, was a major conflict between North and South Korea. China, under Mao's leadership, supported North Korea and sent troops to fight alongside them. This involvement in the Korean War further solidified China's alignment with the Soviet Union and its role as a major player in global affairs.

#### The Sino-Soviet Split

In the late 1950s, tensions between China and the Soviet Union began to rise, leading to a split in their alliance. This split was largely due to ideological differences between Mao and Soviet leader Nikita Khrushchev, as well as China's growing frustration with the Soviet Union's dominance in the communist world. This split had significant implications for China's foreign policy, as it forced China to reevaluate its alliances and alignments.

### Conclusion

Mao Zedong's leadership had a profound impact on China's foreign policy. His ideology of communism and his belief in the power of the masses shaped China's foreign relations during this period. The Cold War, the Korean War, and the Sino-Soviet Split all played a role in shaping China's foreign policy during Mao's reign. As we continue to explore China's foreign policy, it is important to keep in mind the lasting impact of Mao's leadership on China's international relations.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the foreign policy of China during the reign of Mao Zedong. We have seen how Mao's ideology of communism and his belief in the power of the masses shaped China's foreign relations during this period. We have also examined the impact of Mao's foreign policy on China's economic development and international standing.

Mao's foreign policy was characterized by a desire to spread communism and challenge the dominance of Western powers. This led to China's involvement in various conflicts, such as the Korean War and the Sino-Soviet split. However, Mao's foreign policy also had its successes, such as the establishment of diplomatic relations with the United States and the normalization of relations with Japan.

Despite its successes, Mao's foreign policy also had its flaws. The Great Leap Forward and the Cultural Revolution caused significant economic and social disruptions, hindering China's development and international standing. These events also strained China's relationships with other countries, particularly the Soviet Union.

Overall, Mao's foreign policy had a profound impact on China's international relations and its economic development. While it laid the foundation for China's current foreign policy, it also left behind a complex legacy that continues to shape China's role in the global arena.

## Chapter: Chinese Foreign Policy: International Relations and Strategy

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will be exploring the foreign policy of China, specifically focusing on the period of Deng Xiaoping. Deng Xiaoping was a Chinese politician who served as the paramount leader of China from 1978 to 1992. His leadership marked a significant shift in China's foreign policy, as he implemented a series of economic and political reforms that transformed the country into one of the world's largest and most influential nations.

Deng Xiaoping's foreign policy was characterized by a shift towards a more pragmatic and less ideological approach. This was in stark contrast to the previous foreign policy of Mao Zedong, which was heavily influenced by Marxist ideology and resulted in isolation from the international community. Deng's foreign policy aimed to promote economic growth and modernization, while also maintaining China's sovereignty and national interests.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into the key events and decisions that shaped Deng Xiaoping's foreign policy, including his famous "Four Modernizations" plan and his approach to international relations. We will also examine the impact of Deng's policies on China's economic and political development, as well as its relationships with other countries. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of the role of Deng Xiaoping in shaping China's foreign policy and its current position in the global arena.


## Chapter 5: Deng Xiaoping:




### Conclusion

Mao Zedong's foreign policy was a crucial aspect of his leadership and had a significant impact on China's international relations. His approach to foreign affairs was heavily influenced by his ideological beliefs and his desire to establish China as a major global power. Mao's foreign policy was characterized by a combination of pragmatism and idealism, as he sought to balance China's national interests with his vision of a communist world order.

One of the key principles of Mao's foreign policy was the concept of "leaning to one side," which aimed to balance relations with both the Soviet Union and the United States. This approach allowed China to maintain its independence and avoid becoming a pawn in the Cold War rivalry between the two superpowers. Mao also emphasized the importance of non-alignment and anti-imperialism in his foreign policy, which was reflected in China's support for revolutionary movements and anti-colonial struggles around the world.

Mao's foreign policy also had a significant impact on China's relations with its neighbors. His support for North Korea during the Korean War and his involvement in the Vietnam War demonstrated his commitment to defending communist regimes and promoting revolutionary movements. However, his support for the Khmer Rouge in Cambodia and his involvement in the Sino-Soviet split strained China's relations with its neighbors and led to conflicts and tensions.

In conclusion, Mao Zedong's foreign policy was a complex and dynamic aspect of his leadership. It was shaped by his ideological beliefs and his desire to establish China as a major global power. While it had its successes, it also led to conflicts and tensions, particularly in China's relations with its neighbors. Mao's foreign policy continues to be a topic of debate and analysis in the field of international relations, and its impact on China's foreign policy can still be seen today.

## Chapter: Chinese Foreign Policy: International Relations and Strategy

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will be examining the foreign policy of China during the Deng Xiaoping era. Deng Xiaoping was a Chinese politician who served as the paramount leader of China from 1978 to 1992. His leadership marked a significant shift in China's foreign policy, as he implemented a series of economic and political reforms that transformed the country into one of the world's major economic powers.

Deng's foreign policy was characterized by a shift from Mao Zedong's isolationist approach to a more assertive and pragmatic approach. This shift was driven by China's growing economic and military power, as well as its desire to be recognized as a major player in international relations. Deng's foreign policy also aimed to improve China's relations with the United States, which had been strained during the Mao era.

One of the key aspects of Deng's foreign policy was his emphasis on economic development. He believed that a strong economy was crucial for China's modernization and its ability to compete with other major powers. This led to the implementation of the "Four Modernizations" policy, which aimed to modernize China's agriculture, industry, defense, and science and technology.

Another important aspect of Deng's foreign policy was his "peaceful rise" strategy, which aimed to avoid conflict and promote economic growth through peaceful means. This strategy was in contrast to the more aggressive and expansionist policies of other major powers, such as the Soviet Union and the United States.

In this chapter, we will explore the key events and developments that shaped Deng's foreign policy, including his historic visit to the United States in 1979 and his role in the Tiananmen Square protests of 1989. We will also examine the impact of Deng's foreign policy on China's relations with other major powers, such as the United States, the Soviet Union, and Japan.

Overall, this chapter will provide a comprehensive analysis of Deng Xiaoping's foreign policy and its significance in shaping China's role in international relations. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a deeper understanding of the key principles and strategies that guided China's foreign policy during the Deng era.


## Chapter 5: Deng Xiaoping:




### Conclusion

Mao Zedong's foreign policy was a crucial aspect of his leadership and had a significant impact on China's international relations. His approach to foreign affairs was heavily influenced by his ideological beliefs and his desire to establish China as a major global power. Mao's foreign policy was characterized by a combination of pragmatism and idealism, as he sought to balance China's national interests with his vision of a communist world order.

One of the key principles of Mao's foreign policy was the concept of "leaning to one side," which aimed to balance relations with both the Soviet Union and the United States. This approach allowed China to maintain its independence and avoid becoming a pawn in the Cold War rivalry between the two superpowers. Mao also emphasized the importance of non-alignment and anti-imperialism in his foreign policy, which was reflected in China's support for revolutionary movements and anti-colonial struggles around the world.

Mao's foreign policy also had a significant impact on China's relations with its neighbors. His support for North Korea during the Korean War and his involvement in the Vietnam War demonstrated his commitment to defending communist regimes and promoting revolutionary movements. However, his support for the Khmer Rouge in Cambodia and his involvement in the Sino-Soviet split strained China's relations with its neighbors and led to conflicts and tensions.

In conclusion, Mao Zedong's foreign policy was a complex and dynamic aspect of his leadership. It was shaped by his ideological beliefs and his desire to establish China as a major global power. While it had its successes, it also led to conflicts and tensions, particularly in China's relations with its neighbors. Mao's foreign policy continues to be a topic of debate and analysis in the field of international relations, and its impact on China's foreign policy can still be seen today.

This shift was driven by China's growing economic and military power, as well as its desire to be recognized as a major player in international relations. Deng's foreign policy also aimed to improve China's relations with the United States, which had been strained during the Mao era.

One of the key aspects of Deng's foreign policy was his emphasis on economic development. He believed that a strong economy was crucial for China's modernization and its ability to compete with other major powers. This led to the implementation of the "Four Modernizations" policy, which aimed to modernize China's agriculture, industry, defense, and science and technology.

Another important aspect of Deng's foreign policy was his "peaceful rise" strategy, which aimed to avoid conflict and promote economic growth through peaceful means. This strategy was in contrast to the more aggressive and expansionist policies of other major powers, such as the Soviet Union and the United States.

## Chapter 5: Deng Xiaoping:




### Introduction

China's military strategy has been a topic of great interest and concern for the international community. As one of the world's major powers, China's military strategy has a significant impact on global politics and security. In this chapter, we will delve into the contemporary Chinese military strategy, examining its historical roots, key principles, and current applications.

China's military strategy is deeply rooted in its long history of warfare and strategic thinking. The country's military strategy has evolved over centuries, from the ancient times when it was primarily focused on defensive warfare, to the modern era where it has become a major player in global affairs. The Chinese military strategy is also heavily influenced by the country's political and economic policies, as well as its relations with other countries.

One of the key principles of Chinese military strategy is the concept of "people's war". This strategy emphasizes the importance of mobilizing the entire population in times of war, with the belief that the people are the ultimate source of strength and victory. This principle has been a cornerstone of Chinese military strategy for centuries and continues to guide the country's military operations today.

In recent years, China's military strategy has undergone significant changes as the country has risen to become the world's second-largest economy and a major global power. The Chinese military has modernized and expanded its capabilities, with a focus on developing advanced technologies and a blue-water navy. This has raised concerns among other major powers, particularly the United States, about China's growing military power and its potential impact on global security.

In this chapter, we will explore these and other aspects of contemporary Chinese military strategy, providing a comprehensive analysis of the country's military capabilities and operations. We will also examine the challenges and opportunities that China's military strategy presents for the international community, and discuss the implications for global politics and security. 


# Chinese Foreign Policy: International Relations and Strategy

## Chapter 5: Contemporary China: Military Strategy




### Subsection: 5.1a Current Chinese Military Strategy

China's military strategy has been a topic of great interest and concern for the international community. As one of the world's major powers, China's military strategy has a significant impact on global politics and security. In this section, we will delve into the current Chinese military strategy, examining its historical roots, key principles, and current applications.

#### Historical Roots

The roots of China's military strategy can be traced back to its long history of warfare and strategic thinking. The country's military strategy has evolved over centuries, from the ancient times when it was primarily focused on defensive warfare, to the modern era where it has become a major player in global affairs. The Chinese military strategy is also heavily influenced by the country's political and economic policies, as well as its relations with other countries.

One of the key principles of Chinese military strategy is the concept of "people's war". This strategy emphasizes the importance of mobilizing the entire population in times of war, with the belief that the people are the ultimate source of strength and victory. This principle has been a cornerstone of Chinese military strategy for centuries and continues to guide the country's military operations today.

#### Current Applications

In recent years, China's military strategy has undergone significant changes as the country has risen to become the world's second-largest economy and a major global power. The Chinese military has modernized and expanded its capabilities, with a focus on developing advanced technologies and a blue-water navy. This has raised concerns among other major powers, particularly the United States, about China's growing military power and its potential impact on global security.

China's military strategy is also heavily influenced by its relations with other countries, particularly the United States. The two countries have a complex relationship, with both being major global powers and having significant economic and political ties. China's military strategy is designed to protect its national interests and maintain its position as a major global power. This includes a focus on developing advanced military technologies, expanding its military capabilities, and maintaining a strong defense against potential threats.

#### Future Developments

As China continues to rise as a global power, its military strategy will continue to evolve and adapt to new challenges and threats. The country's military modernization efforts will likely continue, with a focus on developing advanced technologies and expanding its military capabilities. China's military strategy will also continue to be heavily influenced by its relations with other countries, particularly the United States. As China becomes a more dominant force in global affairs, its military strategy will play a crucial role in shaping international relations and global security.





### Subsection: 5.1b Role in International Relations

China's military strategy plays a crucial role in its international relations. The country's military power and capabilities have a significant impact on its relations with other countries, particularly the United States. The United States, as the world's largest economy and military power, has been a major focus of China's military strategy.

#### The United States as a Strategic Threat

The United States has been a major strategic threat to China since the end of World War II. The US has been a key player in shaping the global political and economic landscape, and its military presence in the Asia-Pacific region has been a source of concern for China. The US has also been a major critic of China's human rights record and its policies in Tibet and Taiwan, further straining relations between the two countries.

China's military strategy has been designed to counter the US's military power and influence. The Chinese military has been modernizing and expanding its capabilities, with a focus on developing advanced technologies and a blue-water navy. This has raised concerns among other major powers, particularly the United States, about China's growing military power and its potential impact on global security.

#### The United States as a Strategic Partner

Despite the tensions between the two countries, the United States and China have also been strategic partners in many areas. The two countries have a complex relationship, with areas of cooperation and competition. For example, the US and China have been working together to address global issues such as climate change and the North Korean nuclear threat.

However, the US-China relationship has also been marked by competition, particularly in the economic and military spheres. The US has been concerned about China's economic rise and its growing military power, which has led to tensions between the two countries. The US has also been critical of China's human rights record and its policies in Tibet and Taiwan, further straining relations between the two countries.

#### The United States as a Strategic Rival

The United States and China have also been strategic rivals in many areas. The two countries have been competing for influence and power in the global arena, particularly in the Asia-Pacific region. The US has been concerned about China's growing military power and its territorial disputes in the South China Sea, which have led to increased tensions between the two countries.

Despite the tensions, the US and China have also been working together to address global issues such as climate change and the North Korean nuclear threat. However, the competition between the two countries has been a major factor in shaping their relationship, with both countries vying for power and influence in the global arena.

In conclusion, the United States plays a crucial role in China's military strategy and international relations. The US has been a major strategic threat, partner, and rival for China, and their relationship has been shaped by a complex interplay of competition and cooperation. As China continues to rise as a global power, its relationship with the US will continue to be a key factor in shaping its military strategy and international relations.





### Subsection: 5.1c Future Directions

As China continues to rise as a global power, its military strategy will continue to evolve and adapt to new challenges and opportunities. In this subsection, we will explore some potential future directions for China's military strategy.

#### The Rise of Artificial Intelligence

One of the most significant future directions for China's military strategy is the integration of artificial intelligence (AI) into its military operations. China has been investing heavily in AI research and development, and its military has been at the forefront of this effort. The Chinese military has already deployed AI-powered systems for tasks such as surveillance, reconnaissance, and target identification.

In the future, we can expect to see a further expansion of AI capabilities in the Chinese military. This could include the development of autonomous weapons systems, which could revolutionize warfare and give China a significant advantage over its adversaries. However, the use of AI in warfare also raises ethical concerns, and China's approach to these issues will be a crucial factor in shaping its future military strategy.

#### The Role of Cyber Warfare

Another important future direction for China's military strategy is the use of cyber warfare. China has been investing in its cyber capabilities for years, and it has already been accused of engaging in cyber espionage and attacks against other countries. As China's military modernization continues, we can expect to see a further expansion of its cyber capabilities, including the development of offensive cyber warfare capabilities.

The use of cyber warfare presents a unique set of challenges and opportunities for China's military strategy. On one hand, it allows for a more covert and less costly form of warfare, but it also raises concerns about the vulnerability of critical infrastructure and the potential for retaliatory cyber attacks. As China's cyber capabilities continue to grow, it will be crucial for the country to develop a comprehensive cyber strategy that balances these considerations.

#### The Impact of Technological Advancements

Advancements in technology will also play a significant role in shaping China's military strategy in the future. As mentioned earlier, the book "The Next 100 Years: A Forecast for the 21st Century" predicts significant technological advancements for China, including the development of hypersonic aircraft and missiles, military bases on the Moon, and armored robotic battle suits for soldiers.

These advancements could have a profound impact on China's military capabilities and strategy. For example, the development of hypersonic missiles could give China a significant advantage in long-range strikes, while the use of robotic battle suits could enhance the capabilities of its soldiers. However, these advancements also come with their own set of challenges, such as the need for extensive training and the potential for malfunctions.

#### The Role of International Relations

Finally, the future direction of China's military strategy will be heavily influenced by its international relations. As China continues to rise as a global power, it will face increasing competition and potential conflicts with other major powers, particularly the United States. The US-China relationship will continue to be a crucial factor in shaping China's military strategy, as the two countries navigate issues such as trade, human rights, and security.

In addition, China's relations with other countries, particularly its neighboring countries, will also play a significant role in shaping its military strategy. As China's economic and military power continues to grow, it will face increasing pressure to assert its dominance in the region, which could lead to potential conflicts and the need for a more assertive military strategy.

In conclusion, the future directions for China's military strategy are complex and multifaceted. As China continues to rise as a global power, it will face a range of challenges and opportunities that will shape its military capabilities and strategy. The integration of AI, the use of cyber warfare, technological advancements, and international relations will all play a crucial role in shaping China's military strategy in the future.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the military strategy of contemporary China. We have seen how China's military modernization and expansion have been driven by its desire to become a global superpower and to protect its national interests. We have also discussed the key components of China's military strategy, including its focus on asymmetric warfare, cyber warfare, and the development of advanced weapons systems. Additionally, we have examined the challenges and opportunities that China's military strategy presents for the international community.

As China continues to rise as a global power, its military strategy will continue to evolve and adapt to new threats and challenges. It is crucial for the international community to closely monitor and understand China's military strategy in order to effectively engage with it and maintain global stability. The United States, as the current global superpower, will play a crucial role in shaping the future of China's military strategy and its impact on the international community.

# Chinese Foreign Policy: International Relations and Strategy

## Chapter 6: Chinese Perspective on International Organizations




### Subsection: 5.2a South China Sea

The South China Sea is a crucial region for China's military strategy, as it is home to some of the most important shipping lanes in the world and is rich in natural resources. China's military presence in the South China Sea has been a source of tension with its neighbors and the United States, and it will continue to be a key focus of China's military strategy in the future.

#### The South China Sea Dispute

The South China Sea is a contested region, with China, Vietnam, Malaysia, the Philippines, and Brunei all claiming sovereignty over various islands and territories within the sea. China's claims are based on historical and cultural grounds, while its neighbors argue for a more equitable division of the resources and territory.

The dispute has been a source of tension and conflict in the region, with China using its military presence to assert its claims. In recent years, China has been building artificial islands and military installations in the South China Sea, which has raised concerns among its neighbors and the international community.

#### The Role of the South China Sea in China's Military Strategy

The South China Sea is a crucial component of China's military strategy. It is home to some of the most important shipping lanes in the world, and any disruption to these lanes could have significant economic consequences for China. Additionally, the South China Sea is rich in natural resources, including oil and gas, which are essential for China's economic growth.

China's military presence in the South China Sea also serves as a deterrent against potential adversaries. By maintaining a strong military presence, China can deter other countries from challenging its claims and protect its economic interests in the region.

#### Future Directions for China's Military Strategy in the South China Sea

As China continues to rise as a global power, its military strategy in the South China Sea will continue to evolve. China's military modernization efforts, including the development of advanced weapons and technology, will allow it to further assert its claims in the region.

In the future, we can expect to see China continue to build and fortify its military installations in the South China Sea, as well as increase its naval presence in the region. This will likely lead to further tensions and potential conflicts with its neighbors and the United States.

#### The Role of International Law and Diplomacy

The South China Sea dispute is also a significant issue in international law and diplomacy. The United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS) provides a framework for resolving disputes over maritime boundaries and resources. However, China has been reluctant to submit its claims to international arbitration, and its actions in the South China Sea have been met with criticism from the international community.

In the future, China's approach to the South China Sea will likely depend on its relationship with the United States and its neighbors. As China continues to rise as a global power, it may be more willing to engage in diplomatic efforts to resolve the dispute and maintain stability in the region. However, if tensions continue to rise, we may see a more assertive approach from China, leading to potential conflicts and instability in the South China Sea.





### Subsection: 5.2b Taiwan Strait

The Taiwan Strait is another crucial region for China's military strategy, as it is home to one of the busiest shipping lanes in the world and is a potential flashpoint for conflict with the United States. China's military presence in the Taiwan Strait has been a source of tension with the United States and Taiwan, and it will continue to be a key focus of China's military strategy in the future.

#### The Taiwan Strait Dispute

The Taiwan Strait is a contested region, with China and Taiwan both claiming sovereignty over the island. China considers Taiwan to be a part of its territory, while Taiwan sees itself as an independent nation. The dispute has been a source of tension and conflict in the region, with China using its military presence to assert its claims.

The dispute has been further complicated by the United States, which has a long-standing commitment to defend Taiwan. This has led to concerns about the potential for conflict between China and the United States in the Taiwan Strait.

#### The Role of the Taiwan Strait in China's Military Strategy

The Taiwan Strait is a crucial component of China's military strategy. It is home to one of the busiest shipping lanes in the world, and any disruption to this lane could have significant economic consequences for China. Additionally, the Taiwan Strait is a potential flashpoint for conflict with the United States, making it a key focus of China's military strategy.

China's military presence in the Taiwan Strait also serves as a deterrent against potential adversaries. By maintaining a strong military presence, China can deter other countries from challenging its claims and protect its economic interests in the region.

#### Future Directions for China's Military Strategy in the Taiwan Strait

As China continues to rise as a global power, its military strategy in the Taiwan Strait will continue to evolve. China's military modernization and expansion will likely continue, as will its efforts to assert its claims in the region. The potential for conflict with the United States in the Taiwan Strait remains a concern, and China's military strategy will need to adapt to this potential threat.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the contemporary military strategy of China, a rising global power. We have examined the historical context of China's military development, its current capabilities and doctrines, and the future direction of its military strategy. We have also discussed the implications of China's military modernization and expansion for the international community.

China's military strategy is characterized by a focus on asymmetric warfare, cyber warfare, and the development of advanced weapons systems. This strategy is designed to compensate for China's relative lack of conventional military power and to deter potential adversaries. However, as China's economic and military power continues to grow, it is likely to shift towards a more conventional military strategy, with a greater emphasis on traditional military capabilities.

The implications of China's military modernization and expansion are significant. As China becomes a more assertive and capable military power, it is likely to challenge the existing power dynamics in the international community. This could lead to increased tensions and potential conflicts, particularly in the Asia-Pacific region.

In conclusion, understanding China's military strategy is crucial for anyone seeking to understand its foreign policy and international relations. As China continues to rise as a global power, its military strategy will continue to evolve and shape the international landscape.

## Chapter: Chapter 6: Contemporary China: Economic Strategy

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the economic strategy of contemporary China, a country that has been rapidly transforming its economy and asserting its global influence. China's economic strategy has been a key driver of its remarkable economic growth and development, and it has had significant implications for the international community.

China's economic strategy is characterized by a combination of state-led development, market-oriented reforms, and a focus on high-tech industries. This strategy has been guided by the concept of "socialism with Chinese characteristics," which emphasizes the role of the state in guiding economic development while also promoting market competition.

We will explore the key components of China's economic strategy, including its industrial policies, trade policies, and financial policies. We will also examine how these policies have been implemented and their impact on the Chinese economy and the global economy.

Furthermore, we will discuss the challenges and opportunities that China's economic strategy presents for other countries, particularly the United States. As China continues to rise as a global economic power, its economic strategy will have significant implications for international trade, investment, and economic relations.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of China's economic strategy, its evolution, and its implications for the international community. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a deeper appreciation for the complexities of China's economic strategy and its role in shaping the global economic landscape.




### Subsection: 5.2c Cyber Warfare

China's military strategy has also expanded into the realm of cyber warfare, recognizing the growing importance of this domain in modern conflicts. The Chinese military has been investing heavily in developing its cyber capabilities, with the goal of achieving information superiority and gaining an edge over its adversaries.

#### The Rise of Cyber Warfare

The rise of cyber warfare has been a significant development in modern conflicts. With the increasing reliance on technology and the internet, the cyber domain has become a critical battleground, where the control of information can have a decisive impact on the outcome of a conflict. China's military strategy has been quick to adapt to this new reality, recognizing the potential of cyber warfare as a tool for achieving its strategic objectives.

#### China's Cyber Warfare Capabilities

China's cyber warfare capabilities have been steadily growing over the years. The Chinese military has been investing heavily in developing its cyber capabilities, with a focus on offensive operations. This includes the development of cyber weapons and tools for hacking, surveillance, and disrupting enemy systems. The Chinese military has also been working on developing its defensive capabilities, with the goal of protecting its own networks and systems from cyber attacks.

#### The Role of Cyber Warfare in China's Military Strategy

Cyber warfare plays a crucial role in China's military strategy. It is seen as a means to achieve information superiority, which is a key component of modern warfare. By controlling the flow of information, China can gain a strategic advantage over its adversaries, allowing it to make more informed decisions and achieve its objectives more effectively.

Cyber warfare also serves as a means of projecting power and influence beyond China's borders. By conducting cyber attacks against other countries, China can disrupt their systems and cause chaos, demonstrating its capabilities and asserting its power. This has been seen in China's cyber attacks against the United States and other Western countries, which have been aimed at stealing sensitive information and disrupting their systems.

#### Future Directions for China's Cyber Warfare Strategy

As China continues to invest in its cyber capabilities, its cyber warfare strategy is likely to become even more sophisticated and effective. The Chinese military is expected to continue developing its offensive capabilities, with the goal of achieving information superiority in future conflicts. It is also likely to focus on developing its defensive capabilities, to protect its own networks and systems from cyber attacks.

The rise of artificial intelligence (AI) and machine learning (ML) technologies has also opened up new possibilities for China's cyber warfare strategy. These technologies can be used to develop advanced cyber weapons and tools, as well as to automate and enhance the effectiveness of cyber operations. As China continues to invest in these technologies, it is likely to gain a significant advantage in the cyber domain, further enhancing its military capabilities.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the contemporary military strategy of China, a country that has been rapidly modernizing its military capabilities in recent years. We have discussed the key components of China's military strategy, including its focus on asymmetric warfare, cyber warfare, and the development of advanced weapons systems. We have also examined the role of China's military in its broader foreign policy objectives, particularly in the South China Sea and the Taiwan Strait.

China's military strategy is a reflection of its broader foreign policy goals, which include maintaining a peaceful international environment, safeguarding its national interests, and promoting economic development. The Chinese military plays a crucial role in achieving these goals, as it is responsible for protecting China's borders and territorial integrity, as well as for projecting power and influence in the international arena.

As China continues to rise as a global power, its military strategy will continue to evolve and adapt to new challenges and threats. The country's military modernization efforts, coupled with its growing economic and political influence, will likely lead to a more assertive and confident China in the international arena. This will have significant implications for the balance of power in the Asia-Pacific region and beyond.

## Chapter: Chinese Foreign Policy: International Relations and Strategy

### Introduction

In today's globalized world, international organizations play a crucial role in shaping the dynamics of international relations. These organizations serve as platforms for countries to come together and address global issues such as economic development, security, and human rights. China, as one of the major players in the international arena, has a unique approach to participating in these organizations. In this chapter, we will explore the Chinese perspective on international organizations and how it aligns with their foreign policy and strategic goals.

China's involvement in international organizations has significantly increased in recent years, reflecting its growing global influence. From joining the United Nations in 1971 to becoming a founding member of the Shanghai Cooperation Organization in 2001, China has actively participated in various international organizations. This chapter will delve into the reasons behind China's increased involvement and its impact on the global stage.

Furthermore, we will examine the Chinese approach to international organizations, which is guided by their foreign policy and strategic objectives. China's foreign policy is based on the principle of non-interference in the internal affairs of other countries, and this is reflected in its approach to international organizations. We will also discuss how China's strategic goals, such as promoting economic development and safeguarding its national interests, shape its participation in these organizations.

Lastly, this chapter will explore the challenges and opportunities that China's involvement in international organizations presents. As China's global influence continues to grow, its role in these organizations will also increase, bringing both challenges and opportunities. We will discuss how China navigates these challenges and takes advantage of the opportunities presented by its involvement in international organizations.

In conclusion, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of China's approach to international organizations. By examining its involvement, foreign policy, and strategic goals, we can gain insights into how China shapes the global landscape and its role in addressing global issues.

## Chapter 6: Chinese Foreign Policy: International Organizations




### Introduction

China's foreign policy has been a topic of great interest and debate among scholars and policymakers alike. As the world's most populous country and second-largest economy, China's actions have a significant impact on the global stage. In this chapter, we will delve into the contemporary grand strategy of China, examining its key objectives, principles, and tactics. We will also explore how China's grand strategy has evolved over time and the challenges it faces in the ever-changing international landscape.

China's grand strategy is a complex and multifaceted concept, encompassing its overall approach to international relations, economic development, and security. It is shaped by China's unique history, culture, and domestic politics, as well as its position in the global system. As China continues to rise as a global power, its grand strategy will play a crucial role in shaping its relationships with other countries and its position in the world.

In this chapter, we will also examine the key components of China's grand strategy, including its economic policies, military modernization, and diplomatic efforts. We will also discuss the role of China's grand strategy in its relations with the United States, its key economic and military rival. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of China's grand strategy and its implications for the international community.




### Subsection: 6.1a Current Chinese Grand Strategy

China's grand strategy is a crucial aspect of its foreign policy, shaping its relationships with other countries and its position in the global system. In this section, we will explore the key components of China's grand strategy, including its economic policies, military modernization, and diplomatic efforts.

#### Economic Policies

China's economic policies have been a major driving force behind its rapid economic growth and increasing global influence. The country's economic strategy is based on the concept of "socialism with Chinese characteristics," which emphasizes the role of the state in guiding economic development. This approach has allowed China to achieve high levels of economic growth, with an average annual GDP growth rate of 9.5% between 1978 and 2018.

One of the key components of China's economic strategy is its focus on industrialization. The country has invested heavily in infrastructure and manufacturing, creating a strong industrial base that has driven its economic growth. This has also allowed China to become a major exporter, with its exports accounting for a significant portion of its GDP.

In recent years, China has also been promoting its "Made in China 2025" initiative, which aims to upgrade its manufacturing sector and become a global leader in high-tech industries such as artificial intelligence, robotics, and biotechnology. This initiative is part of China's broader goal of achieving economic modernization and becoming a "moderately prosperous society" by 2049.

#### Military Modernization

China's military modernization has also been a key aspect of its grand strategy. The country has been investing heavily in its military, with its defense budget increasing by an average of 10% annually since 1990. This has allowed China to build a modern and capable military, with a focus on developing advanced technologies such as ballistic missiles, aircraft carriers, and stealth aircraft.

China's military modernization has also been driven by its desire to protect its national interests and maintain a peaceful international environment. The country has been actively participating in UN peacekeeping missions and has also been promoting its "peaceful rise" doctrine, which emphasizes the use of non-military means to achieve its goals.

#### Diplomatic Efforts

China's diplomatic efforts have also been a crucial aspect of its grand strategy. The country has been actively engaging with other countries, particularly in Africa and Latin America, to promote economic and political cooperation. China's "Belt and Road Initiative," which aims to connect China with Europe, Africa, and other parts of the world through a network of roads, railways, and ports, is a key example of its diplomatic efforts.

In addition to economic cooperation, China has also been promoting its "win-win" approach to international relations, emphasizing mutual benefits and non-interference in the internal affairs of other countries. This approach has allowed China to build strong relationships with other developing countries, particularly in Africa and Latin America.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, China's grand strategy is a complex and multifaceted concept, encompassing its economic policies, military modernization, and diplomatic efforts. The country's focus on industrialization, military modernization, and diplomatic engagement has allowed it to achieve high levels of economic growth and increase its global influence. As China continues to rise as a global power, its grand strategy will play a crucial role in shaping its relationships with other countries and its position in the world.





### Subsection: 6.1b Role in International Relations

China's role in international relations has been a topic of great interest and debate in recent years. As one of the world's largest and most influential countries, China's actions and policies have a significant impact on the global stage. In this section, we will explore China's role in international relations, focusing on its relationships with other major powers and its approach to global issues.

#### Relationships with Other Major Powers

China's relationships with other major powers have been a key focus of its foreign policy. The country has been actively pursuing a strategy of "peaceful development," which emphasizes the use of non-military means to achieve its goals and promote its interests. This approach has been particularly evident in China's relationships with the United States and Russia.

China's relationship with the United States has been a complex one, with both countries being major economic and military powers. The two countries have had a strong economic relationship, with China becoming the United States' largest trading partner in 2009. However, there have also been tensions between the two countries, particularly in areas such as trade and human rights.

In recent years, there has been a growing concern in the United States about China's economic and military rise. Some have argued that China's economic policies, such as its currency manipulation and intellectual property theft, have contributed to the US-China trade imbalance and have been detrimental to the US economy. There have also been concerns about China's military modernization and its territorial disputes in the South China Sea.

Despite these tensions, both countries have also recognized the importance of maintaining a stable relationship. In 1994, the two countries established a Strategic and Economic Dialogue, which allows for high-level discussions on a range of issues. This dialogue has been crucial in managing the relationship between the two countries and has helped to prevent any major conflicts.

China's relationship with Russia has also been a key aspect of its foreign policy. The two countries have a strong strategic partnership, with both countries being members of the Shanghai Cooperation Organization and the BRICS group. Russia has been a crucial ally for China, particularly in its territorial disputes with the United States and Japan.

However, there have also been some tensions between the two countries, particularly in areas such as energy and trade. Russia has been a major supplier of energy to China, but there have been concerns about Russia's dependence on China for its economic growth. There have also been concerns about China's growing influence in Russia's Far East region.

Despite these tensions, both countries have recognized the importance of maintaining a strong relationship. In 2019, the two countries signed a joint statement reaffirming their commitment to a comprehensive strategic partnership of coordination for a new era. This statement highlighted the importance of cooperation between the two countries in areas such as trade, energy, and security.

#### Approach to Global Issues

China's approach to global issues has also been a key aspect of its foreign policy. The country has been actively involved in addressing global challenges such as climate change, poverty, and health. China has been a major contributor to the United Nations, and has been a key player in international organizations such as the World Health Organization and the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change.

China's approach to global issues has been characterized by a combination of pragmatism and idealism. The country has been a strong advocate for developing countries, particularly in areas such as trade and development. At the same time, China has also been a major contributor to global efforts to address issues such as climate change and health.

In conclusion, China's role in international relations has been a topic of great interest and debate in recent years. Its relationships with other major powers and its approach to global issues have been crucial in shaping its foreign policy. As China continues to rise as a global power, its role in international relations will continue to be a key focus of attention.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the contemporary grand strategy of China in the international arena. We have seen how China's rise as a global power has led to a shift in its foreign policy, with a focus on economic development and regional integration. We have also discussed the challenges and opportunities that this rise presents for China, as well as its impact on the international community.

China's grand strategy is a complex and evolving one, shaped by its domestic and international priorities. As we have seen, China's economic growth has allowed it to become a major player in the global economy, with a significant impact on the world stage. However, this growth has also brought about challenges, such as the need to address income inequality and environmental concerns.

In terms of foreign policy, China's grand strategy has been characterized by a shift towards a more assertive and proactive approach. This has been driven by its desire to secure its economic and political interests, as well as its aspiration to be seen as a responsible global power. China's Belt and Road Initiative, for example, has been a key component of its grand strategy, aimed at promoting regional integration and economic development.

Overall, China's grand strategy is a work in progress, with many challenges and opportunities ahead. As it continues to rise as a global power, it will face increasing scrutiny and pressure from the international community. However, with its economic strength and growing influence, China is well-positioned to shape the future of international relations.
